These days, it\u2019s the baseline for even having a chance. NBA teams that scored 100 or more points lost more often last season than at any time in nearly 35 years \u2014 simply a byproduct of the offensive explosion across the league a year ago, and the continuation of a trend that probably won\u2019t be changing anytime soon. Scoring 100 or more only led to wins 58% of the time in 2018-19, according to a statistical analysis by The Associated Press. That was down from 62% the year before, 70% in 2014-15 and 75% in 2011-12. \u201cI think you have to evolve your thinking, your approach,\u201d Phoenix coach Monty Williams said. \u201cMy rookie year we played a game against Cleveland and the game was in the 70s and that was deemed a really good defensive hard-nosed game. The climate has changed and you have to change with it.\u201d The hard-nosed defensive game Williams speaks of \u2014 at least in the sense of what used to be considered that sort of game \u2014 no longer exists. Minnesota scored 140 points in a non-overtime game against Golden State on Jan. 16 and lost, becoming the first team in 27 years to score that many in a regulation contest and not win. The lowest winning score in the league last year was 89, by Memphis on Nov. 7, 2018 against Denver. Last season, there were an NBA-record 845 instances of a team scoring 100 or more points in a loss. Teams that didn\u2019t score 100 points went 64-385. \u201cAll the rules in today\u2019s NBA favor offense,\u201d Denver coach Michael Malone said. \u201cIt\u2019s becoming harder and harder to guard teams and individuals. But the bottom line is if you want to be a championship-caliber team you have to be able to defend and defend at a high level. The Toronto Raptors won the championship last year \u2014 I think they were fifth in defensive efficiency. You have to find a way. You have to get players to buy in and commit.\u201d There were only 64 instances last season out of 1,230 regular-season games where the winning score was below 100. Teams won 14% of the time when being held to 99 or less \u2014 compared with how they would win 35% of the time seven years ago. Some teams last season simply couldn\u2019t win unless they scored 100: Philadelphia was 0-7 when held to 99 or less, Golden State and New Orleans were both 0-8, Boston and the Los Angeles Clippers were 0-9, the Los Angeles Lakers and Atlanta were 1-10, San Antonio, Brooklyn and Dallas were 1-12, Minnesota and Charlotte were 1-13, Phoenix was 1-22 and New York was 1-28. Memphis was perhaps the \u201cbest\u201d team at winning when scoring less than 100 \u2014 the Grizzlies were 9-28 in those games. \u201cThe pace of the game and the level of skill, everything has gone up,\u201d Dallas coach Rick Carlisle said. \u201cYou\u2019re looking at it with respect to how much you score. The inverse side of that is what you can do to limit the opponent. And that\u2019s a big part of it. The more you limit the opponent and get stops, the greater your chances will be offensively.\u201d The scoring boom that went on last year \u2014 fueled by a seventh consecutive year of record-setting 3-point numbers, faster pace leading to more possessions and the NBA\u2019s crackdown to ensure proper freedom of movement on the offensive end \u2014 isn\u2019t likely to be a one-year thing. A year ago, 20 teams averaged at least 110 points per game. The year before that, only six averaged that much. Consider: Just seven years ago, teams averaged 96 points per game. Last year, it was a smidge over 111 \u2014 the highest number the NBA had seen in nearly 50 years. \u201cThis is crazy,\u201d Heat coach Erik Spoelstra said. \u201cActually, it\u2019s the NBA I grew up with the \u201980s with all these high scores. This game is changing. You\u2019ve got to adapt to it.\u201d * * * AP Sports Writers David Brandt in Phoenix and Schuyler Dixon in Dallas contributed to this report."},{"id":31362,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/anthony-davis-may-play-vs-warriors","date":"2019-10-16","title":"Anthony Davis will return to lineup tonight vs. Warriors","content":"* On ESPN:  Warriors vs. Lakers (10:30 ET) The Los Angeles Lakers got a measure of good news on Monday when they found out that Anthony Davis\u2019 right thumb injury was just a sprain . Teams who longed to build a Big Three are now striving for a dynamic duo, hoping two premier players are good enough to win a championship when the belief used to be that it required a trio. And with some of the combinations assembled this summer, it sure seems less is more. That\u2019s certainly the thinking in Los Angeles, where LeBron James and Anthony Davis will try to power the Lakers back to the playoffs, and Kawhi Leonard and Paul George attempt to carry the Clippers further than they\u2019ve ever gone before. Houston is putting James Harden and Russell Westbrook together in a backcourt of recent MVPs, and Kyrie Irving and Kevin Durant made the Nets the talk of not only New York but also the entire NBA on the opening day of free agency when they decided to partner up in Brooklyn. \u201cEverybody was looking for a Big Three,\u201d George said. \u201cIt didn\u2019t work for a lot of teams, so now this is a new dynamic for the new generation of this league.\u201d One that could make the NBA more balanced and exciting this season. If one team hoards too many good players, as Golden State did while reaching the last five NBA Finals, it leaves a lengthy list of clubs who have no realistic hope of contending for a title. But if it only takes a pair of All-Star players, that opens the door for teams who already have two, or have one and the assets to get another. The Big Three model kept some of those teams from going all in, but that era appears over. \u201cI think that\u2019s the way the league just went,\u201d Davis said. \u201cI\u2019m not sure if it was done purposefully, but I think this is maybe the most exciting the league ever has been in a while. It\u2019s going to be fun.\u201d James won rings with two versions of a Big Three, teaming with Dwyane Wade and Chris Bosh for back-to-back titles in Miami, and then rallying Cleveland to the 2016 championship along with Irving and Kevin Love. Now he\u2019ll try to do it with one fewer, which might be enough if the Lakers put the right pieces around him and Davis, who are two of the top players in the league. So are Leonard and George. Doc Rivers coached a Big Three to the 2008 title in Boston with Kevin Garnett, Paul Pierce and Ray Allen, and the Celtics might\u2019ve won another if not for the Heat building a younger one two years later. Now he\u2019ll try to win with two, with a Clippers team that George thinks looks like many other teams in today\u2019s NBA. \u201cI think what Boston did was special. I think what Miami did was special,\u201d he said. \u201cBut I think it goes back to you have two guys that are the guys and then you have a group around them that supports them to the highest level, and that foundation is around a lot of guys in this league.\u201d Though the focus has been on the tandem of twos, George said a number of teams could point to a complementary player and say they have a Big Three. Houston is one of them, coach Mike D\u2019Antoni said when asked the reason for the trend. \u201cA lot of it is media-driven in the sense of we\u2019ve got Eric Gordon. He\u2019s a superstar,\u201d D\u2019Antoni said. \u201cEric Gordon\u2019s as good as anybody in the league. So you focus on, `Oh, we\u2019ve only got two,\u2019 because that\u2019s the named two. But probably money and cap does create an equal system for everybody.\u201d Trying to afford three top players and keep them for long became even more challenging only a year after James and Bosh went to Miami, with the 2011 Collective Bargaining Agreement implementing severe penalties for teams who exceed the luxury tax. Short of players sacrificing salary, as Tim Duncan repeatedly did in San Antonio when the Spurs kept him, Tony Parker and Manu Ginobili together long enough to win four titles, it\u2019s almost impossible to get three stars and a competent cast around them. Even if the salaries work, the personalities might not. It\u2019s hard to keep three alpha dogs happy, as Irving showed by asking out of Cleveland, especially because one of them usually has to give up so much of his game so three isn\u2019t a crowd. \u201cYou have a guy that\u2019s used to being a focal point of the offense \u2013 take Bron\u2019s teams in Miami,\u201d Lakers coach Frank Vogel said. \u201cThere\u2019s more sacrifice I think probably to be had if it\u2019s a Big Three versus a Big Two, but that would be my two cents on it.\u201d And his two cents on the two-man trend? \u201cI think we\u2019ve got the best of them, of all the duos,\u201d he said."},{"id":31370,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/jimmy-butler-heat-hopes-soar","date":"2019-10-16","title":"JImmy's era: Butler arrives in Miami, Heat hopes soar","content":" MIAMI (AP) \u2014 Jimmy Butler wants this made clear: He is not in Miami to be the next Dwyane Wade. The Kings open the regular season Oct. 23 at Phoenix."},{"id":31404,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/coaches-challenge-other-rules-changes-2019-20","date":"2019-10-11","title":"Coach's challenge could be a timely tool for teams to wield","content":" Cleveland\u2019s John Beilein, the only new-to-the-league coach this season, actually got a jump on his 29 rivals in one department. To better familiarize himself with the Cavaliers team he was taking over, Beilein broke from the tradition that has assistant coaches working the sideline at NBA Summer League. When the situation arose in a game in Las Vegas for Cleveland to invoke the experimental \u201ccoaches\u2019 challenge\u201d rule , Beilein was the one calling for it. And the one getting shot down. \u201cIt was an out-of-bounds play,\u201d Beilein recalled during a break at the coaches\u2019 meetings in Chicago last month. \u201cMy player came to the bench saying, \u2018It\u2019s definitely our ball.\u2019 I thought, \u2018Great, this is why we have it now.\u2019 \u201cWe came back out. It was their ball.\u201d  There will be a lot of dashed hopes in 2019-20, as well as some pivotal reversals, with the NBA\u2019s adoption of the latest replay wrinkle . As in MLB and the NFL, coaches will have the opportunity to appeal, in real time, certain referees\u2019 decisions. All the \u201ctriggers\u201d of the existing replay system remain, but now the teams will have a sense of control. One time each game. \u201cI\u2019ve been a proponent of it for many years, just as an additional layer of security,\u201d said Dallas coach Rick Carlisle, who also serves as president of the NBA Coaches Association . \u201cIf a call\u2019s inaccurate for any reason, it\u2019s just an extra chance \u2014 particularly if the game\u2019s on the line \u2014 to get it right. \u201cThe question has always been, how to execute it. Where to start. Sounds like this is going to start with a high level of simplicity. Then we\u2019ll see where it goes.\u201d Denver Nuggets coach Michael Malone thought back to 2017-18, when the Nuggets missed the postseason after a loss at Minnesota in the season\u2019s final game . Like every game, there were a handful of what-if moments. \u201cThink about it,\u201d Malone said. \u201cTwo years ago, one play could have been the difference for us between the lottery and playoffs. That saves jobs, that gets home\/road seeding, there are a lot of things that it can affect.\u201d For this season, the challenge can be made in three situations : to question a foul called against that team\u2019s player, to dispute an out-of-bounds decision or to question a goaltending\/basket interference ruling against that team. The first type applies to the entire game; the others to the first 46 minutes (and first three minutes of overtime), after which the established triggers take over. Challenging a call requires the coach to first call a timeout and then inform the referees he wants a review. There are new court administrators at every game this season to help with the process. Also, fans will notice green \u201cchallenge lights\u201d at the scorer\u2019s table \u2014 the one nearest the challenging bench will blink. Beilein said he sought redress a couple of times in Las Vegas, without satisfaction. \u201cThey never reversed their decisions,\u201d he said, \u201cbut it\u2019s really a good idea to do, to let us have this say in a game. You ask, they review it. If they don\u2019t see it, you just move on with the game. It puts things away, so we\u2019re not grinding away all night on that call. It\u2019s over. It\u2019s done.\u201d If a call is reversed, the challenge is successful and the team\u2019s timeout is restored. If the initial ruling stands, the challenge is deemed unsuccessful and that timeout is gone. Win or lose the appeal, the allotment stays the same: One challenge per team per game. The early chatter among coaches has been, when is the best time to use it? In Sunday\u2019s Hornets-Celtics game, Brad Stevens and James Borrego waited until the final minute . Both challenges failed.  \u201cI\u2019ll probably save it till the fourth quarter,\u201d Lakers coach Frank Vogel said. \u201cI\u2019m going to be really excited about it when it helps wins me some games. And I\u2019m going to really hate it when it costs me.\u201d Said Malone: \u201cThe funny thing is, we always say, \u2018The game never comes down to just the last play. Something that happened in the first quarter was just as important.\u2019 But the reality it, when you get to the last two minutes, if you have the coaches challenge in your pocket, that could come up with a really big play or give you momentum.\u201d The refs\u2019 crew chief will have the final determination of fouls. He or she also will be able to \u201cclean up\u201d the play in question if, for instance, they notice the foul was assessed incorrectly or if a different foul by either side occurred before the one being reviewed. Note: infractions such as 3-second violations or traveling, if uncalled initially, can\u2019t be assessed in a challenge review. The league\u2019s Replay Center in Secaucus, N.J., will adjudicate out-of-bounds and goaltending challenges. At the NBCA September meetings in Chicago, the feature \u2014 also given a trial run in the G League in recent seasons \u2014 was discussed in a ballroom session with referees and supervisors of the officials. The next day, they all spent time on a basketball court, walking through the particulars. Borrego took advantage of his proximity in Charlotte to talk with Carolina Panthers coach Ron Rivera about his strategy in using the NFL\u2019s version . Those coaches physically throw a red flag to signal their challenge and have time to hear from assistant coaches in a stadium booth upstairs who have seen video to determine their chances of reversal. The NBA won\u2019t have either flags to throw or helpers checking. The coaches will have to alert the refs by twirling their fingers in the air, the current universal symbol for \u201creplay.\u201d They\u2019ll need to act before an opposing player is handed the ball to shoot free throws or toss it inbounds, or before a jump ball. \u201cWe haven\u2019t had this conversation with them yet, but players never think they fouled,\u201d Milwaukee Bucks coach Mike Budenholzer said Monday. \u201cIt\u2019s never out on them. We\u2019re gonna have to say, \u2018OK, did you really not foul?\u2019 Somehow figure out, \u2018OK, you have to tell the truth.\u2019 \u201cThat kind of feedback from them is going to be important in a challenge situation.\u201d The preseason was only a few days old but, in this era of analytics, Chicago Bulls coach Jim Boylen had his crew gather data on every early challenge. He\u2019s working up a list of situations in which to use it. Late in games? Sure. But not so late that the existing triggers take over for a disputed out-of-bounds play. Then the coach might go home without using it. \u201cYou\u2019re always concerned about [burning] the timeout,\u201d Boylen said. \u201cYou\u2019d better be sure. Your [viewing] angles better be good.\u201d Not everyone is a fan of the experiment, which will be evaluated after the season by the NBA\u2019s Competition Committee. Some skeptics fret that adding reviews will mean more delays in games that already have replay interruptions. Then there was Monty Williams, the Phoenix Suns\u2019 new coach. Part of his dislike? Genuine empathy for the referees. \u201cI\u2019m not a fan of it at all,\u201d Williams said. \u201cSometimes it\u2019s to your detriment, but I think human error is part of our game. I know we\u2019re trying to get it right, but sometimes [replay] causes referees to get second-guessed a lot. They already are. \u201cAnd this is just one more thing for coaches to have to do. Now we\u2019re all going to have to delegate a guy on our bench to monitor things.  \u201cIf we\u2019re gonna challenge, I wish it was a segment \u2014 say, the last three minutes of the game. I want to coach. I don\u2019t want to be focused all night on, \u2018Should I have challenged [a call made earlier]?\u2019 \u201d Fans might notice other rules changes and priorities for officials this season: * Coaches will be required to submit their starting lineups earlier now , making them public at least 30 minutes before tipoff. This change is seen largely as a nod to the looming arrival of legal sports betting. Knowing the starters earlier \u2014 and which regulars might be sitting out with injuries or for \u201cload management\u201d \u2014 means more wagers can be made with the most updated information. (A change still can be made if a player gets hurt or aggravates an injury during warm-ups.) * The Replay Center will take over determinations of 2-pointers vs. 3-pointers , operating automatically. * There figure to be a spate of traveling calls early this season. The referees have made that infraction one of their \u201cPoints of Education\u201d for 2019-20. That means a \u201cmore stringent enforcement\u201d of the existing rule, according to Monty McCutchen, the NBA\u2019s VP, head of referee development and training. The league has gone so far as to include the concept of \u201cthe gather\u201d in its rule book now. That \u2014 the moment when a player has full control of the ball and thus the point from which he can take two steps \u2013 has been used for years by game officials. But now it has been codified, which helps when discerning variations such as steps taken backward (rather than in forward progress) or in the \u201cEuro-step.\u201d McCutchen noted that, in years past, the NBA game was played through the post at a slower pace. Referees evaluated plays starting with the defenders. Now, with hand-checking long gone and 3-pointers pulling players farther out on the court, the refs\u2019 sequence of viewing plays has shifted to feet, then release, then defender. Other Points of Education for the refs this year have focused on illegal contact initiated by offensive players, \u201cfreedom of movement\u201d issues and \u201crespect for the game\u201d moments, which basically are emotional overreactions to calls that exceed allowable guidelines. * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. "},{"id":31347,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/pacers-exercise-options-tj-leaf-aaron-holiday","date":"2019-10-10","title":"Pacers exercise options on Leaf, Holiday","content":" INDIANAPOLIS (AP) \u2014 The Indiana Pacers have exercised contract options on TJ Leaf and Aaron Holiday. Indiana also signed veteran guard Walt Lemon Jr., who played with Boston, Chicago and New Orleans over the past two seasons."},{"id":31406,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/game-changers-ben-sullivan-giannis-trainer","date":"2019-10-10","title":"How great becomes greater","content":" Editor\u2019s Note:  This is one in a series of features that spotlights innovative, unique figures around the NBA who could change the league forever. * * * MILWAUKEE \u2014 More than two hours before tipoff, Giannis Antetokounmpo already is working. Ten minutes after that, he\u2019s tugging at the shoulder strap of his white athletic shirt, wiping sweat from his face. Fans aren\u2019t even in the building yet as Antetokounmpo grinds through a battery of shooting drills. He starts out basic, flipping a yellow, weighted ball through the rim as he takes a step backward after each shot. Then the Milwaukee Bucks\u2019 star and the NBA\u2019s reigning Kia MVP moves through specific stations \u2014 foul line, baselines, mid-range wings \u2014 while a few teammates shoot at the same end of the court. A cluster of staffers handle the rebounds. The music in the arena bowl is loud. Photographers are setting up their equipment. Eventually the anthem singer comes out for a trial run. There are, frankly, a couple dozen possible distractions at Fiserv Forum more than two hours before the Jazz-Bucks preseason game Wednesday. And yet, as far as Antetokounmpo is concerned, it\u2019s just him, the ball, the rim \u2026 and Bucks assistant coach Ben Sullivan. Sullivan is the tall, bearded fellow in team gear who puts the franchise\u2019s guy through these paces before games, after practices, sometimes on random weekend nights in the offseason when Antetokounmpo feels the itch to work. The term they use in Milwaukee is \u201ctaking your vitamins\u201d \u2014 it\u2019s a daily commitment. For all the attention heaped on the NBA\u2019s global vision this week, Antetokounmpo and Sullivan represent one of its upsides, bringing together an earnest, soft-spoken native of Portland, Ore., and a ridiculously gifted young man from Athens, Greece. Thirty years ago, they might never have met. These days, they labor at all hours in various gyms, to little fanfare, in hopes of delivering a championship to an underdog organization in a town where some folks wear cheese hats made of foam. That\u2019s for later. The shorter-term goal is simple: Constant improvement by Antetokounmpo, at the prodding and watch of a guy who needed a day job seven years ago because his coaching gig wasn\u2019t paying the bills. \u201cI could work with him for another 20 years,\u201d Sullivan said shortly after Antetokounmpo\u2019s shooting session, \u201cand we\u2019d still be working on this stuff.\u201d As he said it, he looked heavenward, made the sign-of-the-cross and smiled. When you\u2019re living the dream, you don\u2019t want to jinx a thing. \u201cHe\u2019s a great shooting coach,\u201d Antetokounmpo said, \u201cbut he\u2019s not just a shooting coach. We work on my post game. We work on my ball skills. Just becoming a better overall player.\u201d They got to the NBA a year apart. Antetokounmpo was a skinny, knobby kneed 18-year-old who spoke halting English and had been considered a reach by former Bucks GM John Hammond as the No. 15 pick in the 2013 Draft . Now, \u201cThe Greek Freak\u201d ranks as one of the top three or four players in the NBA, a basketball-without-borders success story to rival those of NBA legends Hakeem Olajuwon , Dirk Nowitzki or Yao Ming . The Bucks are pinning their hopes for an NBA title on Antetokounmpo\u2019s talents, work ethic and relentless pursuit of greatness. Sullivan? He was a 28-year-old then, wrapping up his first NBA season breaking down game film in the San Antonio Spurs\u2019 video room. The season before, he stayed busy driving from his home in Portland up to Vancouver, Wash., to work as an office manager for a construction firm. Come 3 p.m., he\u2019d head back down to Lewis & Clark College as an assistant coach for the Pioneers , the Division III school\u2019s hoops team. Without the first job, Sullivan \u2014 a graduate of the University of Portland who played professionally for a spell in Germany, China and Chile \u2014 couldn\u2019t have afforded to give so much time to the second.  Now the Bucks are pinning their hopes for an NBA title on Sullivan\u2019s talents, work ethic and relentless pursuit of Antetokounmpo\u2019s greatness too. However deep into spring Milwaukee plays, the contributors will range from \u201cThe Greek Freak\u2019s\u201d elite skills and MVP notoriety to Sullivan\u2019s relative anonymity (you might glimpse him in sideline shots sitting a few seats away from Bucks coach Mike Budenholzer). \u201cGiannis is such a great player,\u201d Budenholzer said this week, \u201cthe more we can push that envelope of him continuing to improve, that\u2019s super-important to us. And that\u2019s a great strength of Ben\u2019s. He\u2019s got a good way of helping players take progressive steps in their games. A lot of times, if we have somebody where we\u2019re spending extra time on shooting, he gets tasked with the majority of that.\u201d If it sounds as if Budenholzer has Sullivan\u2019s back, there\u2019s a reason: they climbed the same ladder, learned the NBA game the same way, in the same place, and plugged their way to all they\u2019ve achieved. Budenholzer, out of Pomona College, famously was hired by the Spurs to work in their video department a quarter century ago after pestering Gregg Popovich for the opportunity. He worked his way onto the bench, was around for four championships, then got hired by the Atlanta Hawks in 2013 for a five-year run. Sullivan had been dumped in Budenholzer\u2019s lap the year before he left, hired by GM R.C. Buford with a recommendation from player-turned-coach Ime Udoka (Udoka and Sullivan were fixtures on the Portland hoops scene.) The timing couldn\u2019t have been better for Sullivan \u2014 the Spurs went to The Finals that season and the next, suffering from Ray Allen\u2019s famous corner 3-pointer in Game 6 in \u201913 , then beating Miami for rings in \u201914 . When Budenholzer got an opening that summer, he called Sullivan. \u201cThe dream was always to just get a job in coaching that could pay my bills,\u201d Sullivan recalled. \u201cIt didn\u2019t happen till I got to the NBA.\u201d Initially, Sullivan wasn\u2019t sure whether to heed Udoka\u2019s advice, so he sought a second opinion. He didn\u2019t need a third. Said Sullivan: \u201cI had a coach I\u2019m close to tell me, \u2018Do whatever you have to do to get that job. The amount of stuff you\u2019ll learn and the people you\u2019ll meet, it could be the best thing to ever happen to you. If you have to take out a loan just to finance it, do that.\u2019 When he said that, I knew this was something I needed to do.\u201d The video department of an NBA team is like the boiler room of a luxury cruise ship. It\u2019s as unglamorous as the league gets, with long hours spent in windowless rooms into the wee hours. Few of the coordinators and assistant coordinators plan to be in those jobs very long. And the reason they can dream like that is, what they learn there makes them so valuable in other roles.  \u201cYou really learn to see the game and train your eye in a much, much different way,\u201d Budenholzer said. \u201cLots of times you actually have to see all 10 guys on the court and understand, are they doing what they\u2019re supposed to be doing? Sometimes you have to be creative and think, what is it they were supposed to be doing? \u201cI can tell you, those video guys are truly trained to see the spacing, the timing, how offenses progress, what are teams doing defensively. There\u2019s an attention to detail that you learn in the video room that, I don\u2019t want to say you can\u2019t get anywhere else, but it\u2019s a huge part of their foundation.\u201d  Frank Vogel, coach of the Los Angeles Lakers, got his start in Rick Pitino\u2019s video room at Kentucky and later when Pitino coached the Boston Celtics. Colleagues from Sullivan\u2019s Spurs stint are scattered on benches throughout the NBA: Bret Brielmaier in Brooklyn, Will Hardy out front in San Antonio, Chris Babcock with Philadelphia. They grab the lowest rung on the coaching ladder, then the next and the next. The climb is made possible by the know-how, the feel, the networking, all gleaned from that demanding, immersive environment.  \u201cGoing into the Spurs\u2019 video room was like getting your doctorate in basketball,\u201d Sullivan said. \u201cYou\u2019re not doing it for the money. You\u2019re doing it for the knowledge and the basketball experience.\u201d Budenholzer\u2019s call from Atlanta got Sullivan climbing. His first season with the Hawks, the team won a franchise-record 60 games while he worked with proven NBA players, including current Bucks shooter Kyle Korver. Atlanta reached the playoffs three times in his four seasons. When Budenholzer parted ways with the franchise in 2018 and snagged the Bucks job , Sullivan was among the staffers he brought to Milwaukee . Sullivan mentions coaches in both San Antonio and Atlanta \u2014 Chip Engelland (with the Spurs), Chad Forcier (now with the Bucks) and Kenny Atkinson (now the Nets\u2019 coach) \u2014 as mentors. When asked what he\u2019s learned or borrowed from Engelland, one of the league\u2019s most respected \u201cshot doctors,\u201d the Bucks assistant said: \u201cEverything. Like, am I going to get sued for plagiarism? The amount of stuff I learned from him and Pop and Bud, I could never repay.\u201d He can, however, pay it forward in his tutelage of Antetokounmpo. All he must do is help a reigning MVP still shy of his prime at just 24 improve on the performance he gave in 2018-19 and boost his team to six additional playoff victories next spring. That\u2019s a lot to ask from a player who averaged 27.7 points, 12.5 rebounds and 5.9 assists per game, while hitting 57.8 percent of his shots and earning All-Defensive first team honors . Might as well try to tweak the smile on the Mona Lisa, right?  \u201cHe still has a lot of growth,\u201d Sullivan said, \u201cand that\u2019s what gets me excited. His passing, his handle, his pick-and-roll play, his one-on-one play. Playing off the ball. Then on defense, he can be more engaged. He has some areas he can really grow in. I think that\u2019s scary for everyone else.\u201d \u201cHe wants it, man. He wants to be great. He wants to drive us. He wants this to be the last team standing. He\u2019s a driven man.\u201d The job as Antetokounmpo\u2019s sorta-kinda guru\/tutor\/coach was open because the fellow who\u2019d previously held it, Sean Sweeney, got cleared out when the Jason Kidd\/Joe Prunty regime ended in 2018 . Sweeney, who had been devoted to Antetkounmpo\u2019s development since arriving with Kidd in 2014, works now for Detroit. No one spent more time working with \u201cThe Greek Freak\u201d than Sweeney over his four seasons in Milwaukee. They forged a relationship that transcends team affiliation, though logistics and their daily demands keep them apart these days. Thinking of those years, Antetokounmpo said of his helpers then and now: \u201cThey\u2019re similar. One thing I\u2019ll say about Sweeney is, he literally lived in the gym. He didn\u2019t go back home. Ben has kids [Penny and Walker with wife Bailee]. But whenever I\u2019m texting him \u2014 \u2018Coach, meet me at 8:30 or 9 p.m.\u2019 \u2014 he\u2019s always there. Ready.\u201d  Building a new relationship with a player, particularly a superstar, can challenge an assistant coach. Trust is the cornerstone. Can the coach really help the player improve? Does the player even want to be coached? \u201cIn my experience,\u201d Sullivan said, \u201cif you are genuine and passionate and real, if you have good attention to detail and you\u2019re focused, I think if you bring that to the table, players will respond and respect that.\u201d And if they don\u2019t, well, no one wants to be the assistant coach who pushed the franchise star too hard, for fear it might push him away altogether. Though that apparently isn\u2019t a worry for the Bucks with Antetokounmpo.  \u201cThat\u2019s another thing that makes Giannis special,\u201d Sullivan said. \u201cHis desire to be great. And then his willingness to be coached \u2014 one of the best I\u2019ve ever seen. He knows what he has inside, and he knows he needs help. Bud coaches him, I coach him, [fellow assistant] Darvin [Ham] coaches him. He\u2019s that driven to be better. He wants to be pushed.\u201d The way Antetokounmpo works through his warm-ups and tutorials, a coach is more likely to tire out himself than wear out the versatile 6-foot-11 star. While Sullivan observes, assistant video coordinator Schuyler Rimmer \u2014 a former Florida forward who stands 6-foot-10 \u2014 joins in to play post defense against the Bucks\u2019 star. There\u2019s a whole lot of banging going on. \u201cEvery player has holes in his game,\u201d Antetokounmpo told NBA.com. \u201cYou try to fill all holes up as much as you can. Ben is doing a great job. \u201cI definitely want to be challenged by my coaches. I told Sullivan that. I said, \u2018Coach, tell me what I\u2019m supposed to do and I\u2019ll try to do it as hard as I can. Do not try to be nice with me. Just fire away. No BS. Tell me what to do. That\u2019s how we\u2019re going to get better. If I\u2019m BS-ing in a practice, tell me right to my face.\u2019 \u201d Sound harsh. What about a coach\u2019s bedside manner, doesn\u2019t that matter? \u201cBeing nice?\u201d Antetokounmpo said. \u201cThere are times when you\u2019ve got to be nice. But in sports, you\u2019ve got to be real. Sometimes when you\u2019re real, that hurts. But if it\u2019s for your own good or the team\u2019s good, it doesn\u2019t really hurt. Because I want to get better. Help me get better.\u201d That gets us to the Holy Grail of Antetokounmpo\u2019s burgeoning game: His shooting. It\u2019s the one skill that, as league GMs and coaches have said for years now, lags behind the rest of his blossoming on both sides of the ball. If he were to boost his jump shot to a reliable accuracy and stretch it to (gasp) 3-point range, he might be downright unstoppable. Antetokounmpo\u2019s field-goal success is built largely from his dunks and baskets at the rim. Once he\u2019s out to three feet or farther, he shoots at about a 35% rate . He\u2019s a career 27.7% 3-point shooter, which includes 25.6% last season. There still are awkward moments when opponents leave Antetokounmpo unattended \u2014 if he has picked up his dribble, anyway \u2014 almost daring him to shoot from mid-range and beyond. As Milwaukee lost the final four games to Toronto in the Eastern Conference finals in May, the 3-pointers he did launch ( he went 7-of-21 ) seemed to come from the frustration of running into the Raptors\u2019 defensive wall. Conventional wisdom says Antetokounmpo shouldn\u2019t need much of a jump shot, given Milwaukee\u2019s many 3-point threats. The idea, after all, is to spread the defense primarily so he has lanes to the basket. That happened last season, with the Bucks ranking second in the NBA in both 3-pointers taken and made . But Sullivan flipped the thinking. \u201cHis shooting is not just for him, it\u2019s also for his teammates,\u201d Sullivan said. \u201cIf Eric Bledsoe and Khris [Middleton] are in the pick-and-roll and Giannis is in the corner, if they kick it to Giannis, that\u2019s a situation where he has to be ready and willing to take that shot. He\u2019s physically capable of making it, no problem, but the mental aspect has to be \u2018This is a shot I\u2019m gonna do.\u2019 \u201d The public notices the mechanics of Antetokounmpo\u2019s jump shot, which often looks to be just that: mechanical. He lifts the ball high, out in front of his face more than above his head. Tilting back, he looks as if he\u2019s peeking over a fence. When he sends it toward the rim, the fingers of his enormous right hand are spread wide in follow-through. None of that is a problem in Sullivan\u2019s view, though it\u2019s still a little paint-by-numbers. \u201cI\u2019m not sure if I heard this somewhere or where it came from,\u201d the coach said, \u201cbut shooting is a balance of fluidity and structure. All the great shooters have some sort of balance. We\u2019re trying to carve out and find the perfect balance for Giannis, what his fluidity and structure is.\u201d  His real breakthrough will come when outside shots are equal options in Antetokounmpo\u2019s repertoire. \u201cThe term I\u2019ve heard is \u2018shooting psyche,\u2019 \u201d Sullivan said. \u201cEven more so than technique, I think mentally he\u2019s really starting to understand the impact [his shooting] can have on everybody. When Giannis comes in, he\u2019s going to the rim and trying to dunk on people. Which is right. So deciding to shoot is secondary. \u201cBut he needs to mentally grow in that area, of \u2018attack with the jumper, shoot a pull-up here, shoot a three there.\u2019 We want him to play completely to his strengths, and then whenever a shot\u2019s available, take it.\u201d By the way, Sullivan isn\u2019t only a shooting coach. He works with other Bucks players, same as Antetokounmpo gets coached by Budenholzer and his other assistants. \u201cBen\u2019s always out there, working as hard as any guy on the court,\u201d said center Brook Lopez, who stretched his 3-point range with Sullivan\u2019s aid last season. \u201cHe lets guys get out there and kind of experiment, thinking how to elevate their game. He doesn\u2019t have you doing the same stuff to be the same player. He really helps people become better players.\u201d And yes, that includes a dominant player who is being measured mostly against himself and, OK, maybe some legends. \u201cHe\u2019s going to continue to improve because that\u2019s who he is,\u201d Lopez said of Antetokounmpo. \u201cHe\u2019s not satisfied at all with the player he currently is. He sees himself as someone who can be a whole \u2018nother player by leaps and bounds.\u201d As Antetokounmpo\u2019s daily regimen of catch-and-shoots, off-the-dribble 3-pointers, pull-ups and post moves ended, he made sure to seek out and slap hands with each of the staffers and ball kids who helped rebound for him. When this all comes together in Antetokounmpo as both an inside and outside scoring threat \u2014 and it might happen more quickly than folks anticipate \u2014 the highest five will belong to Sullivan. * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. All of the usual media sessions surrounding the Lakers-Nets preseason game in Shanghai on Thursday \u2014 including a scheduled news conference from NBA Commissioner Adam Silver and postgame news conferences with the teams \u2014 have been canceled. It\u2019s the latest salvo in the rift between the league and China stemming from a since-deleted tweet posted last week by Morey, the general manager of the Houston Rockets. \u201cThere will be no media availabilities for tonight\u2019s game between the Brooklyn Nets and Los Angeles Lakers,\u201d the NBA said in a statement Thursday, released a few hours before the game. The game was proceeding as scheduled. Fans arriving at the arena to watch \u2014 many of them donning NBA jerseys \u2014 were handed small Chinese flags to carry with them inside, and at least one person carried a sign critical of Silver. \u201cI understand that there are consequences from that exercise of, in essence, his freedom of speech,\u201d Silver said at a news conference in Tokyo earlier this week. \u201cWe will have to live with those consequences.\u201d And this move was one of those consequences. Morey\u2019s tweet expressed support for anti-government protesters in Hong Kong, and sparked fallout that has completely overshadowed the NBA\u2019s annual trip to China \u2014 which typically takes on a celebratory tone. Not this year. Most events in advance of the game, such as NBA Cares events to benefit educational causes and the Special Olympics, were called off, as was a \u201cfan night\u201d where Lakers and Nets players were to interact directly with some Chinese ticketholders. Signage in Shanghai to promote the game \u2014 huge photos of LeBron James, Anthony Davis, Kyrie Irving and other players \u2014 was ripped down, and mentions of the game were scrubbed from the arena website. All that comes as many Chinese corporations suspended their business ties to the NBA. Chinese state broadcaster CCTV said it was not going to show the Lakers-Nets games on Thursday or in Shenzhen on Saturday, and NBA broadcast partner Tencent also said it was changing its coverage plans for the league. First, as an undersized, little-recruited point guard who dominated in college at Colorado State. Then as an undersized, undrafted point guard who enjoyed one of the most decorated careers in WNBA history. But the South Dakota native outdid even herself in 2014, when a chance meeting with Gregg Popovich, and the unofficial internship that resulted, prompted the Spurs to hire her as the first full-time female assistant coach in the history of North America\u2019s four major professional leagues. Here are 10 things to know about her improbable journey. Make or miss \u2026 and walk: A deadly shooter, Hammon credits some of her prowess to her formative years, where she spent countless hours hooping on a rim mounted over a large concrete slab at the family home just outside Rapid City in the Black Hills National Forest. In addition to honing her stroke, she had extra motivation to make her jumpers count: To retrieve errant shots, she had to trudge down the hill from the Hammon homestead. \u201cI didn\u2019t have any neighbors,\u201d Hammon told Bleacher Report in 2014 , \u201cand if you missed, the ball rolled for 30 yards.\u201d Playing up: In addition to working obsessively on her shot, Hammon played almost exclusively against boys leading up to high school, including youth leagues in which she often played several years above her own age group. Despite her lack of size, quickness and hops, the experience proved essential to her future success, primarily in learning how to get off and make creative shots over larger, more athletic opponents. \u201cA couple of pump fakes and throw it with some twist off the board,\u201d father Marty Hammon told Bleacher Report . \u201cThat just comes from playing with boys. We always had to find a way of getting that shot off. Where for anybody else that was crap, for her it was necessary. That was part of her game.\u201d Queen of the Hills : All that work paid off when Hammon arrived at Stevens High School, where she set career records for points, assists and steals while winning a pair of state player of the year awards. Former Stevens coach Ron Riherd, who led the school for more than 20 seasons, called her \u201cby far the best player I ever coached.\u201d Yet Hammon still agonizes over the final contest of her high school career, a loss in the state semifinals in which the opposing team hit a game-winning buzzer-beater after she missed the front end of a one-and-one. Hammon, who would retire with the WNBA\u2019s best career free-throw percentage at 89.7, finished with 29 of her team\u2019s 34 points in the game. Mr. and Mrs. Basketball: Hammon was preceded by several years at Stevens by Eric \u201cThe Polish Rifle\u201d Piatkowski, who starred at Nebraska before enjoying a 14-year NBA career with the Clippers, Rockets, Bulls and Suns. The life aquatic: An avid outdoorswoman, Hammon received a shotgun for her 14th birthday. While hunting wasn\u2019t quite her thing \u2014 her brother Matt told Bleacher Report she cried after shooting her first (and only) deer \u2014 Hammon has an abiding fascination with the ocean, especially sharks. \u201cBecky loves those big sharks,\u201d said Marty Hammon, whose family takes an annual vacation in Key West. \u201cAnd they\u2019re never big enough for her.\u201d Unwanted: Despite her high school achievements, Hammon was almost entirely overlooked by college recruiters. It wasn\u2019t until the summer before her senior year, at a camp in Indiana, that an assistant coach from nearby Colorado State \u2014 there to scout other players \u2014 couldn\u2019t help but notice the short, slow point guard who kept schooling the so-called superstars. \u201cShe made them look silly,\u201d that assistant, Kari Gallegos-Doering, told Bleacher Report . \u201cShe could just do things and go around people and make passes. I remember a player running down the floor shaking her head. She had just been duped. That was Becky. Here\u2019s a nobody making these players look bad.\u201d That nobody went on to re-write the record books at Colorado State, setting school marks in career points, assists and 3-pointers while making three All-America teams and winning the 1999 Pomeroy Naismith Award, given annually to the best women\u2019s senior under 5-foot-8.  History repeats: Just as college recruiters showed little interest in the 5-foot-6 Hammon, neither did professional scouts put much credence in her extraordinary career at Colorado State. And just as Hammon took maximum advantage of that opportunity, so did she prove her doubters wrong yet again in the WNBA. Undrafted in 1999, Hammon would enjoy one of the most decorated careers in league history, highlighted by six All-Star appearances, four All-WNBA selections, the league record for career assists and selection as one of the WNBA\u2019s Top 15 Players of All Time after 14 total seasons with the New York Liberty and the San Antonio Stars. \u201cHer size never mattered,\u201d Richie Adubato, her former coach with the Liberty, told the New Yorker in 2018 . \u201cWhen she drove to the basket, it didn\u2019t matter who was in there. She had one shot blocked, I think, in four years.\u201d Circuitous route: In addition to her professional success, Hammon was also a two-time Olympian. Just not for her birth nation. Unable to get even an invitation to try out for the U.S. Olympic team in 2008, Hammon made the unusual \u2014 and highly controversial \u2014 decision to instead become a citizen of Russia, where she played professionally in the WNBA offseason for CSKA Moscow. Critics weren\u2019t shy, with then-U.S. coach Anne Donovan questioning Hammon\u2019s patriotism and star center Lisa Leslie refused to shake her hand after the two nations met in the tournament semifinals. But Hammon put her hard-earned mental toughness to good use, shaking off the heat to help Russia win the bronze medal with 22 points against China before representing the country again at the 2012 Games.  \u201cI took a beating,\u201d Hammon told the New Yorker . \u201cIt helped build something inside me.\u201d Unofficial audition: It was with the Stars, for whom Hammon played from 2007 to 2014, that her relationship with Popovich began. The two first spent meaningful time together on their flight back from those 2012 Olympics, with Popovich peppering her with questions and Hammon impressing with her intelligence and candor. Popovich\u2019s admiration for Hammon was further elevated when she spent the 2013 season with the Spurs in an unofficial capacity while she rehabbed from a knee injury. Quiet at first, Hammon gradually began participating more and more in practices and film studies, even challenging Popovich when she saw fit. \u201cThat\u2019s when I knew,\u201d Popovich told the New Yorker , \u201cif I had the opportunity, I wanted to put her on staff.\u201d Said former Spurs player Pau Gasol: \u201cBecky Hammon can coach NBA basketball. Period.\u201d The climb continues: Hammon\u2019s star has only continued to rise since her hiring by the Spurs. Indeed, the Milwaukee Bucks even interviewed her for their vacant head coaching position in 2018. Several years before that, she helped the Spurs win the 2015 Las Vegas Summer League championship as the first female head coach in that competition. She currently serves alongside Spurs legend Tim Duncan as one of Popovich\u2019s top assistants. "},{"id":31553,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/nba-china-relationship-morey-tweet","date":"2019-10-08","title":"Adam Silver says he still plans to visit China later this week","content":"NBA Commissioner Adam Silver said the league is not apologizing for Houston Rockets general manager Daryl Morey\u2019s since-deleted tweet showing support for anti-government protesters in Hong Kong, even after China\u2019s state broadcaster canceled plans to show a pair of preseason games in that country later this week. Silver, speaking Tuesday at a news conference in Tokyo before a preseason game between the Rockets and NBA champion Toronto Raptors, went as far as to say that he and the league are \u201capologetic\u201d that so many Chinese officials and fans were upset by Morey\u2019s tweet and comments that followed \u2014 but insisted that Morey has the right to freedom of expression . > Adam Silver\u2019s statement on NBA, China \u201cDaryl Morey, as general manager of the Houston Rockets, enjoys that right as one of our employees,\u201d Silver said. \u201cWhat I also tried to suggest is that I understand there are consequences from his freedom of speech and we will have to live with those consequences.\u201d Among those consequences: CCTV said it would not show the games between the Los Angeles Lakers and Brooklyn Nets, who will play Thursday in Shanghai and Saturday in Shenzhen. Basketball is wildly popular in China and those two teams \u2014 largely because of LeBron James starring for the Lakers and Chinese e-commerce giant Alibaba\u2019s co-founder Joe Tsai now owning the Nets \u2014 almost certainly would have been a huge television draw. \u201cWe\u2019re strongly dissatisfied and oppose Adam Silver\u2019s claim to support Morey\u2019s right to freedom of expression,\u201d CCTV said in a statement. \u201cWe believe that any remarks that challenge national sovereignty and social stability are not within the scope of freedom of speech.\u201d The broadcaster is also reviewing all its cooperation and exchanges involving the NBA, said the statement posted to CCTV Sports\u2019 official social media account. The NBA is not the first major corporation to deal with criticism from China over political differences. Mercedes-Benz, Delta Air Lines, hotel operator Marriott, fashion brand Zara and others also have found themselves in conflicts with China in recent years. Silver is going to Shanghai on Wednesday and said he hopes to meet with officials and some of the league\u2019s business partners there in an effort to find some sort of common ground. He said he hopes Chinese officials and fans look at the totality of the impact of the three-decade-plus relationship between the league and their country, and urged them to see his response while acknowledging there are political differences between the countries. \u201cI\u2019m sympathetic to our interests here and our partners that are upset,\u201d Silver said. \u201cI don\u2019t think it\u2019s inconsistent on one hand to be sympathetic to them and at the same time stand by our principles.\u201d Silver said the NBA did not expect CCTV to cancel plans to show the Lakers-Nets games. \u201cBut if those are the consequences of us adhering to our values, I still feel it\u2019s very, very important to adhere to those values,\u201d Silver said. The rift between China and the NBA started late last week when Morey tweeted a now-deleted image that read: \u201cFight for Freedom. Stand with Hong Kong,\u201d in reference to months of pro-democracy demonstrations in the semiautonomous Chinese territory that has been mired in escalating violence between protesters and law enforcement. Efforts were quickly made to defuse the impact; Rockets owner Tilman Fertitta said Morey does not speak for the Rockets, and Morey returned to Twitter on Monday in an effort to clarify his meaning. But damage was clearly done: At least one Chinese sporting goods company said it was no longer cooperating with the Rockets, NBA streaming partner Tencent \u2014 which has a $1.5 billion contract with the league over the next five seasons \u2014 said it would not show Rockets games and a sports news website in China said it was no longer covering the team. Further complicating the matter is this: China\u2019s best-known basketball player is Hall of Famer Yao Ming, who spent his NBA career with the Rockets. Yao is now the president of the Chinese Basketball Association, which has said it is suspending its relationship with the Rockets as part of the response to Morey\u2019s tweet. The CBA also canceled plans to have the G League affiliates from Houston and Dallas play preseason games in China later this month. \u201cI\u2019m hoping that together Yao Ming and I can find an accommodation,\u201d Silver said. \u201cBut he is extremely hot at the moment, and I understand it.\u201d A ceremonial NBA Cares event for an educational center in Shanghai with the Nets was canceled Tuesday, though the Nets participated in other events as scheduled. The Lakers were arriving there Tuesday. Silver said NBA Cares would still honor its bigger mission surrounding that event, including providing a gift of new computers. \u201cHow can it be possible to carry out exchanges and cooperation with China without knowing China\u2019s public opinion?\u201d Chinese Foreign Ministry Spokesman Geng Shuang said Tuesday. \u201cNBA\u2019s cooperation with China has been going on for quite a long time, so for what should be said and what should be done, they know best.\u201d Silver also released a statement before his news conference, saying \u201cthose who question our motivation\u201d should know that the NBA\u2019s stance is about more than business. Many U.S. lawmakers have called for the league to take action \u2014 some even suggesting the games in China should be canceled by the NBA. \u201cWe are not apologizing for Daryl exercising his freedom of expression,\u201d Silver said. \u201cI regret \u2014 again, having communicated directly with many friends in China \u2014 that so many people are upset, including millions and millions of our fans. Let me be more clear. O ver the last three decades, the NBA has developed a great affinity for the people of China. We have seen how basketball can be an important form of people-to-people exchange that deepens ties between the United States and China. I n fact, one of the enduring strengths of the NBA is our diversity \u2014 of views, backgrounds, ethnicities, genders and religions. A short time after Morey posted that statement, the NBA said it was \u201cregrettable\u201d that the deleted tweet offended many in China. And all that followed several companies in China, including some of the NBA\u2019s major business partners there, lashing out over Morey\u2019s original tweet. Morey tweeted an image that read \u201cFight for Freedom. Stand with Hong Kong.\u201d referring to the four-month-old protests in the semi-autonomous Chinese territory. That led to Houston owner Tilman Fertitta turning to Twitter to say that Morey does not speak for the Rockets, and sparking an outcry that included the Chinese Basketball Association \u2014 whose president is Yao Ming, the former Rockets star center \u2014 saying it was suspending its relationship with the team. Other criticism came from Tencent, a major media partner of the NBA in China with a streaming deal that is worth $1.5 billion over the next five years, and Chinese state television \u2014 both of which said they would not be showing Rockets games. It wasn\u2019t immediately clear if Morey\u2019s new tweets or the NBA\u2019s statement that followed would be enough to salvage those relationships. Chinese athletic apparel maker Li-Ning released a statement saying it was upset with Morey\u2019s tweet. \u201cI did not intend my tweet to cause any offense to Rockets fans and friends of mine in China,\u201d Morey tweeted early Monday from Japan, where Houston is playing this week. \u201cI was merely voicing one thought, based on one interpretation, of one complicated event. I have had a lot of opportunity since that tweet to hear and consider other perspectives. \u201cI have always appreciated the significant support our Chinese fans and sponsors have provided and I would hope that those who are upset will know that offending or misunderstanding them was not my intention. My tweets are my own and in no way represent the Rockets or the NBA.\u201d NBA Chief Communications Officer Mike Bass said the league recognizes that Morey\u2019s tweet \u201cdeeply offended many of our friends and fans in China, which is regrettable.\u201d Bass added that the league supports individuals \u201csharing their views on matters important to them.\u201d \u201cWe have great respect for the history and culture of China and hope that sports and the NBA can be used as a unifying force to bridge cultural divides and bring people together,\u201d Bass said. Our presence in Tokyo is all about the promotion of the @NBA internationally and we are NOT a political organization.\u201d The timing of this is particularly awkward for the NBA, whose players have often been outspoken on social issues in the United States. China has teams in the U.S. playing preseason games this week, the Rockets are about to play two games in Japan and the Los Angeles Lakers \u2014 with one of the biggest global sports stars in LeBron James \u2014 and Brooklyn Nets set to play Thursday in Shanghai and Saturday in Shenzhen, China. Speaking at a practice in Tokyo on Monday, Rockets\u2019 star James Harden was contrite as he spoke standing with teammate Russell Westbrook. \u201cWe apologize. We love everything there about them and we appreciate the support that they give us individually and as organization.\u201d Nets owner Joe Tsai is a co-founder of Chinese e-commerce giant Alibaba, and posted an open letter on his Facebook page late Sunday night saying Morey stepped on what he described as \u201ca third-rail issue\u201d when it comes to China and Hong Kong. \u201cBy now I hope you can begin to understand why the Daryl Morey tweet is so damaging to the relationship with our fans in China,\u201d Tsai wrote. \u201cI don\u2019t know Daryl personally. I am sure he\u2019s a fine NBA general manager, and I will take at face value his subsequent apology that he was not as well informed as he should have been. Democratic presidential candidate Julian Castro, a former U.S. Housing Secretary from Texas, tweeted \u201cChina is using its economic power to silence critics \u2014 even those in the U.S.\u201d Fostering strong relationships with China has been a priority of the NBA for at least three decades. The NBA has a China office, just announced plans to add a gaming team in Shanghai to the NBA 2K League, and officials in both countries say as many as 500 million Chinese watched at least one NBA game last season. Several NBA players \u2014 including major current and former stars like Stephen Curry and Kobe Bryant \u2014 go to China annually to promote their individual brands, and the World Cup held in China this summer saw countless fans attending in NBA jerseys. The Rockets, largely because of Yao, have an enormous Chinese following. China accuses foreign parties in the U.S. and elsewhere of encouraging the demonstrations. The protests that started in June over a now-shelved extradition bill have since snowballed into an anti-China campaign amid anger over what many view as Beijing\u2019s interference in Hong Kong\u2019s autonomy that was granted when the former British colony returned to Chinese rule in 1997. Odds are, there have been days when Presti has felt even older, given the ups and downs, successes and failures, injuries, personnel comings and goings, and assorted headaches that come with his job. After a strong run through his first 12 seasons \u2013 a 558-410 (.576) regular-season record, nine playoff appearances in the past 10 years, four trips to the Western Conference finals and a loss in five games to Miami in the 2012 Finals \u2013 Presti is facing the first true rebuild on his watch. Gone are Kevin Durant and Russell Westbrook, who earned league MVPs with OKC. So are All-Stars and big names such as James Harden (who became an MVP with Houston), Paul George, Serge Ibaka, Victor Oladipo and Carmelo Anthony. With all eyes on newcomer point guard Chris Paul as the next star Presti might trade in the coming months, here are 10 things to know about the man who will make that call (and countless others) for the Thunder again in 2019-20: \u2018C\u2019 student made good: Presti was born in Concord, Mass., an only child whose parents divorced when he was a teenager. A 2017 Sports Illustrated profile noted that he wasn\u2019t much of a student at Concord-Carlisle Regional High, but eventually got traction when he spent time with a group of mostly African-American kids bused from neighborhoods far from his suburb and the teacher (Harriett Stevens) who supervised them. As a basketball player, Presti impressed his teammates enough to earn the nickname \u201cBob Sura,\u201d after the Florida State product who played 10 NBA seasons. Teamwork, defense came early : Presti enrolled at Virginia Wesleyan and spent two years there before transferring to Emerson College as a way to get back to Boston. He served as a team captain in his junior and senior seasons, establishing himself as a player willing to chase 50\/50 balls and draw as many charges as possible. He also coaxed teammates to agree that, if one of them wasn\u2019t working hard enough, the others could vote that guy off the team. Of Presti\u2019s work ethic, Emerson coach Hank Smith told The New York Times in 2010 : \u201cThat\u2019s how he\u2019s been since the day I met him. He\u2019ll be like that today, tomorrow, the next time I talk to him \u2026 How does anyone drive themselves that hard, that long?\u201d A break, an internship, an NBA career: How did Presti get his big break? A teammate at Emerson got hurt and was thinking of quitting. Presti talked him into continuing. That player\u2019s father was the superintendent of schools in Aspen, Colorado. Every summer, San Antonio coach Gregg Popovich and GM R.C. Buford ran a basketball camp there. They needed one more camp counselor. The superintendent thought of the young man who had helped his son. Presti scraped up money to make the trip, and on the final day persuaded Buford to take him back to San Antonio as an intern. His salary? $250 a month. The opportunity? Priceless. Tony Who? Presti soon impressed Buford, Popovich and others in the Spurs organization with his energy and diligence. He tackled all the usual menial intern tasks, both basketball-related and otherwise. Buford later said, only partly in jest, that he might have learned as much from the Emerson kid as the kid learned from the Spurs exec. He was promoted quickly up the ranks in San Antonio, where his signature moment came in 2001. By then serving as assistant GM, poring over videos with Buford, Presti encountered a point guard from France who hadn\u2019t shown well in his workout. But Presti edited together a highlight reel of the guard\u2019s play, influencing the bosses to bring him back for a second look. Tony Parker \u2014 six-time NBA All-Star, four-time NBA champion and 2007 NBA Finals MVP \u2014 was still on the board when the Spurs made the final pick of the first round. The boss\u2019 choice : Lenny Wilkens, Hall of Fame player and coach, was serving as SuperSonics president in 2007 when the team needed a new GM. So he interviewed former Orlando exec John Gabriel and Atlanta assistant GM Gary Fitzsimmons. Meanwhile, team owner Clay Bennett \u2014 who had been a partner in the Spurs \u2014 interviewed Presti by himself. Guess whose opinion mattered most. Bennett even demoted Wilkens to \u201cvice chairman\u201d to eliminate any doubt about Presti\u2019s newfound power. Some in Seattle saw Presti \u2014 incidentally, the youngest GM in the league and one of nine at the time who had never played in the NBA \u2014 as the owner\u2019s puppet or front man, hired to take flak and run interference as the franchise was prepped for its relocation to OKC. He had upset many fans soon after taking the job by trading popular guard Ray Allen to Boston and forward Rashard Lewis to Orlando. Others, after meeting the so-called whiz kid, saw it differently. Jerry Brewer, a columnist for The Seattle Times , wrote that Bennett \u201cdidn\u2019t just hire some GM. He hired the absolute best man to step into this situation and thrive. He just ensured his franchise will be a contender within four years, possibly sooner.\u201d By then, the Sonics had morphed into the Thunder. MV3: Three weeks after Presti got his GM job in Seattle, he grabbed Kevin Durant second in the 2007 Draft, right after Portland took eventual flop Greg Oden. A year later, he zagged when others zigged, picking UCLA\u2019s Russell Westbrook fourth overall. In 2009, Memphis\u2019 choice of Hasheem Thabeet left James Harden on the board for Presti at No. 3. That trio not only helped OKC to the Finals in 2012, but also and perhaps even more impressively, earned the NBA\u2019s highest individual honor \u2014 Most Valuable Player \u2014 in 2014 (Durant), 2016 (Westbrook) and 2017 (Harden). No NBA exec has ever pulled off anything comparable. The (first) one who got away: In hindsight, Presti\u2019s 2012 trade of Harden to Houston \u2014 a pre-emptive move in deference to a looming tax bill, the bearded guard\u2019s contract demands and a belief that Ibaka would be more valuable \u2014 looks like a clear mistake. The Thunder haven\u2019t reached the Finals since, and of the pieces OKC got back, only Steven Adams (as a 2013 pick) brought long-term value to the Thunder. No one anticipated the cap soon would balloon by more than 60 percent (from $58 million to $94 million) in the four years after Harden\u2019s trade. Fifteen first-round picks \u2013 15! \u2013 by 2026: Presti, on behalf of the Thunder, voted against the NBA\u2019s recent revision of the draft lottery odds, flattening the chances of landing the top pick to discourage tanking. Smaller-market teams such as his lean more heavily on the draft for talent, with free agents less interested in signing there. No surprise then, when Presti did start his rebuild, that he prioritized picks over current players. Here is what the Thunder essentially have available over the next seven years (allow for some minor protection). And this is with Paul, Danilo Gallinari and others as trade bait before next spring: \u2022 Two 2020 first-round picks \u2022 Two 2021 first-round picks \u2022 Two 2022 first-round picks \u2022 Two 2023 first-round picks \u2022 Three 2024 first-round picks \u2022 One 2025 first-round pick \u2022 Three 2026 first-round picks Bang on the drum all day: When you get death threats in your day job, as Presti did after trading George to the Clippers this summer, it\u2019s important to have ways to decompress. In his case, that often means throwing on a pair of headphones and attacking the drum set he has played since his youth. A longtime jazz fan, Presti and friends recorded three CDs that were sold to benefit a children\u2019s charity in Boston. He burned mix CDs for coach Mike Brown when they both were in San Antonio, and consulted with famed musical brothers Branford and Wynton Marsalis about the parallels between jazz and basketball. Presti also is into meditation, sticks to a diet that\u2019s way healthier than yours and spends as much time as he can with his wife Shannon, son Nicholas and twin daughters Millie and Elise. And when he can, he\u2019ll binge on music documentaries that, for example, show how albums such as Pink Floyd\u2019s \u201cThe Wall\u201d or Jay-Z\u2019s \u201cReasonable Doubt\u201d came together. Following in his footsteps: Picture a lanky white guy, a Massachusetts native, playing hoops at Emerson College. He lands a gig as an intern with the San Antonio Spurs, rises through the ranks, then moves to the NBA team headquartered in Oklahoma City. Seen this movie, right? Except that this time, it\u2019s not Presti. It\u2019s Rob Hennigan, former Orlando GM who traveled an eerily similar path about five years after Presti. Hennigan, from Worcester, Mass., played for the same basketball coach at Emerson, Hank Smith. He spent four years with the Spurs, then was brought by Presti to Oklahoma City for another four. Shaking the young-visionary tree, the Magic hired Hennigan as Otis Smith\u2019s replacement in 2012. Despite similarities, Hennigan\u2019s boy-wonder term didn\u2019t produce results on par with Presti\u2019s. He oversaw the Magic\u2019s basketball operation for five seasons, during which the team compiled a 132-278 record, had three head coaches and never made the playoffs. Hennigan is back with the Thunder, re-hired by Presti with the new-agey title of \u201cVice President of Insight & Foresight.\u201d"},{"id":31524,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/first-preseason-coach-challenge-luke-walton-sacramento-kings","date":"2019-10-06","title":"Luke Walton first to win coach's challenge in preseason game","content":" Officially, it\u2019s an NBA first: Coaches successfully argued referees\u2019 calls Saturday. Sacramento\u2019s Luke Walton became the first coach to win a challenge \u2014 a new option for coaches to use in the NBA this season \u2014 when he asked for a review of an out-of-bounds call during the third quarter of the Kings\u2019 game against the Indiana Pacers in Mumbai, India. Review showed that Walton was right and the Kings retained possession. So Mumbai becomes the answer to a trivia question \u2014 the site of the first successful NBA coaches\u2019 challenge. Coaches may challenge one call per game, provided what they\u2019re challenging meets certain criteria and if they have a timeout remaining. T he Lakers roster stands at 20, including two two-way players."},{"id":31502,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/mike-dantoni-tries-out-coaches-challenge-preseason","date":"2019-10-04","title":"Mike D'Antoni tries out new coach's challenge rule vs. Clippers","content":" The Houston Rockets improved to 2-0 in the preseason early Friday morning, drubbing the LA Clippers 109-96 in Honolulu. Houston star James Harden played 28 minutes and shot only 8 of 22 from the field and 4 for 14 from 3-point range, but hit 17 of 20 free throws to finish with 37 points . He also committed an offensive foul in the first half that Houston coach Mike D\u2019Antoni tried to overturn by using the one challenge coaches get per game this season. Replay showed that Harden hooked his defender\u2019s arm with his right arm while he dribbled with his left hand, so the call was upheld. After the game, D\u2019Antoni took a lighthearted approach to his using of the challenge and said he wasn\u2019t immediately sure the call was wrong when he decided to challenge it. \u201cIt\u2019s easy to screw up,\u201d D\u2019Antoni said, per ESPN\u2019s Tim MacMahon . \u201cIt\u2019s going to be a little adjusting. It takes a while to get used to. We don\u2019t have the regular flow of information that we\u2019ll have in a regular game. They\u2019ll tell me before that I should go out there and challenge.\u201d The coach\u2019s challenge is being tested by the NBA on a one-year trail. Starting this season (and in the preseason), each team has one challenge per game. The coach can challenge any personal foul called against his team throughout the game. In the first 46 minutes of the game (or the first 3 minutes of overtime), the coach can challenge a goaltending or basket interference call against his team or an out of bounds where his team is not awarded possession. To perform a coach\u2019s challenge, a team must call timeout prior to one of the following: the ball being given to a free-throw shooter, the ball being given to a player throwing the ball in or a referee tossing a jump ball. The coach signals for a challenge by twirling his index finger upward \u2014 similar to the replay signal \u2014 which will then be confirmed by the official if it is within the challenge rules. From there, a blinking \u201cchallenge light\u201d will signal courtside as the play is reviewed. D\u2019Antoni and LA Clippers coach Doc Rivers both said after Thursday\u2019s preseason game that their teams\u2019 analytics staffs have encouraged them to not look to save their challenges for end-of-game scenarios. \u201cYou know, it\u2019s going to be something that is going to take a little bit for us to get used to and when to use it,\u201d Rivers said, per ESPN. \u201cMy guess is we are going to use it first half, if it\u2019s one of your better players that you think didn\u2019t commit a foul, you may use it to make sure he gets the foul back. Analytically they said we should use it anytime which I don\u2019t agree with that. We\u2019ll see how it goes.\u201d Information from The Associated Press was used in this report."},{"id":31547,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/kawhi-george-out-clippers-preseason-opener","date":"2019-10-04","title":"Kawhi Leonard, Paul George to sit out Clippers' preseason opener","content":" The Clippers\u2019 two-star debut will wait at least one more preseason game. Jovan Buha of The Athletic reports that coach Doc Rivers plans to sit Kawhi Leonard and Paul George in the team\u2019s preseason opener against the Rockets at Honolulu. Leonard chose the Clippers as his new team during the offseason free agency period after leading the Toronto Raptors to their first NBA championship. George leaped at the chance to join the Finals MVP in Los Angeles, requesting a trade from Oklahoma City that ultimately netted the Thunder the largest haul of draft picks in NBA trade history. Without their new stars, the Clippers will be headlined by defending Kia Sixth Man of the Year Lou Williams and energetic big man Montrezl Harrell. The Rockets are coming off a 140-71 preseason shellacking of the Shanghai Sharks on Monday. James Harden led the Rockets in the rout, earning a triple-double before halftime and finishing with 10 points, 12 rebounds and 17 assists in just 21 minutes of action."}]
